   <h1 class="firstHeading" id="firstHeading" lang="en">
    Epigenetics
   </h1>
   <div class="mw-body-content" id="bodyContent">
    <div id="siteSub">
     From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia
    </div>
    <div id="contentSub">
    </div>
    <div class="mw-jump" id="jump-to-nav">
     Jump to:
     <a href="#mw-head">
      navigation
     </a>
     ,
     <a href="#p-search">
      search
     </a>
    </div>
    <div class="mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en">
     <div class="hatnote">
      For the unfolding of an organism or the hypothesis that plants and animals (including humans) develop in this way, see
      <a href="/wiki/Epigenesis_(biology)" title="Epigenesis (biology)">
       epigenesis (biology)
      </a>
      .  For epigenetics in robotics, see
      <a href="/wiki/Developmental_robotics" title="Developmental robotics">
       developmental robotics
      </a>
      .  For the scientific journal, see
      <a href="/wiki/Epigenetics_(journal)" title="Epigenetics (journal)">
       Epigenetics (journal)
      </a>
      .  For earth science concepts labelled
      <i>
       epigenetic
      </i>
      , see
      <a class="mw-disambig" href="/wiki/Epigenetic_(earth_sciences)" title="Epigenetic (earth sciences)">
       epigenetic (earth sciences)
      </a>
      .
     </div>
     <p>
      In
      <a href="/wiki/Genetics" title="Genetics">
       genetics
      </a>
      ,
      <b>
       epigenetics
      </b>
      is the study of cellular and physiological trait variations that are
      <i>
       not
      </i>
      caused by changes in the
      <a href="/wiki/DNA" title="DNA">
       DNA
      </a>
      sequence; in layman's terms, epigenetics is essentially the study of external or environmental factors that turn genes
      <i>
       on
      </i>
      and
      <i>
       off
      </i>
      and affect how cells
      <i>
       read
      </i>
      genes.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-1">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        1
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Hence, epigenetic research seeks to describe dynamic alterations in the
      <a href="/wiki/Transcriptional_regulation" title="Transcriptional regulation">
       transcriptional
      </a>
      potential of a
      <a href="/wiki/Cell_(biology)" title="Cell (biology)">
       cell
      </a>
      . These alterations may or may not be
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Heritable" title="Heritable">
       heritable
      </a>
      , although the use of the term "epigenetic" to describe processes that are not heritable is controversial.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nature2008_2-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-nature2008-2">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        2
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Unlike
      <a href="/wiki/Genetics" title="Genetics">
       genetics
      </a>
      based on changes to the DNA sequence (the
      <a href="/wiki/Genotype" title="Genotype">
       genotype
      </a>
      ), the changes in
      <a href="/wiki/Gene_expression" title="Gene expression">
       gene expression
      </a>
      or
      <a href="/wiki/Cell_(biology)" title="Cell (biology)">
       cellular
      </a>
      <a href="/wiki/Phenotype" title="Phenotype">
       phenotype
      </a>
      of epigenetics have other causes, thus use of the prefix
      <i>
       epi-
      </i>
      (Greek:
      <i>
       
      </i>
      - over, outside of, around).
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3">
       <a href="#cite_note-3">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        3
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4">
       <a href="#cite_note-4">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        4
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      The term also refers to the changes themselves: functionally relevant changes to the
      <a href="/wiki/Genome" title="Genome">
       genome
      </a>
      that do not involve a change in the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nucleotide_sequence" title="Nucleotide sequence">
       nucleotide sequence
      </a>
      . Examples of mechanisms that produce such changes are
      <a href="/wiki/DNA_methylation" title="DNA methylation">
       DNA methylation
      </a>
      and
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Histone_modification" title="Histone modification">
       histone modification
      </a>
      , each of which alters how genes are expressed without altering the underlying
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/DNA_sequence" title="DNA sequence">
       DNA sequence
      </a>
      . Gene expression can be controlled through the action of
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Repressor_protein" title="Repressor protein">
       repressor proteins
      </a>
      that attach to
      <a href="/wiki/Silencer_(DNA)" title="Silencer (DNA)">
       silencer
      </a>
      regions of the DNA. These epigenetic changes may last through
      <a href="/wiki/Cell_division" title="Cell division">
       cell divisions
      </a>
      for the duration of the cell's life, and may also last for multiple generations even though they do not involve changes in the underlying
      <a href="/wiki/DNA" title="DNA">
       DNA
      </a>
      sequence of the organism;
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid17522671_5-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-pmid17522671-5">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        5
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      instead, non-genetic factors cause the organism's genes to behave (or "express themselves") differently.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6">
       <a href="#cite_note-6">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        6
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      One example of an epigenetic change in
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eukaryotic" title="Eukaryotic">
       eukaryotic
      </a>
      biology is the process of
      <a href="/wiki/Cellular_differentiation" title="Cellular differentiation">
       cellular differentiation
      </a>
      . During
      <a href="/wiki/Morphogenesis" title="Morphogenesis">
       morphogenesis
      </a>
      ,
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Totipotent" title="Totipotent">
       totipotent
      </a>
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Stem_cells" title="Stem cells">
       stem cells
      </a>
      become the various
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pluripotent" title="Pluripotent">
       pluripotent
      </a>
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cell_line" title="Cell line">
       cell lines
      </a>
      of the
      <a href="/wiki/Embryo" title="Embryo">
       embryo
      </a>
      , which in turn become fully differentiated cells. In other words, as a single fertilized egg cell  the
      <a href="/wiki/Zygote" title="Zygote">
       zygote
      </a>
       continues to
      <a href="/wiki/Mitosis" title="Mitosis">
       divide
      </a>
      , the resulting daughter cells change into all the different cell types in an organism, including
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Neurons" title="Neurons">
       neurons
      </a>
      ,
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Muscle_cells" title="Muscle cells">
       muscle cells
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Epithelium" title="Epithelium">
       epithelium
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Endothelium" title="Endothelium">
       endothelium
      </a>
      of
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Blood_vessels" title="Blood vessels">
       blood vessels
      </a>
      , etc., by activating some genes while inhibiting the expression of others.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid17522676_7-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-pmid17522676-7">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        7
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
     </p>

     <p>
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Definitions_of_term">
       Definitions of term
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Epigenetics&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Definitions of term">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Historical_usage">
       Historical usage
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Epigenetics&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Historical usage">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      <i>
       Epigenetics
      </i>
      (as in "epigenetic landscape") was coined by
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Conrad_Hal_Waddington" title="Conrad Hal Waddington">
       C. H. Waddington
      </a>
      in 1942 as a
      <a href="/wiki/Portmanteau" title="Portmanteau">
       portmanteau
      </a>
      of the words
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Epigenesis_(biology)" title="Epigenesis (biology)">
        epigenesis
       </a>
      </i>
      and
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Genetics" title="Genetics">
        genetics
       </a>
      </i>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-waddington_8-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-waddington-8">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        8
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <i>
       Epigenesis
      </i>
      is an old
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-OED1_9-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-OED1-9">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        9
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      word that has more recently been used (see
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Preformationism" title="Preformationism">
        preformationism
       </a>
      </i>
      for historical background) to describe the differentiation of cells from their initial
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Totipotent" title="Totipotent">
       totipotent
      </a>
      state in embryonic development.
     </p>
     <p>
      When Waddington coined the term the physical nature of genes and their role in heredity was not known; he used it as a conceptual model of how genes might interact with their surroundings to produce a
      <a href="/wiki/Phenotype" title="Phenotype">
       phenotype
      </a>
      ; he used the phrase "epigenetic landscape" as a metaphor for
      <a href="/wiki/Morphogenesis" title="Morphogenesis">
       biological development
      </a>
      . Waddington held that
      <a href="/wiki/Cell_(biology)" title="Cell (biology)">
       cell
      </a>
      fates were established in development much like a marble rolls down to the point of
      <a href="/wiki/Local_optimum" title="Local optimum">
       lowest local elevation
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Waddington2014_10-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Waddington2014-10">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        10
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Waddington suggested visualising increasing irreversibility of cell type differentiation as ridges rising between the valleys where the marbles (cells) are travelling.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11">
       <a href="#cite_note-11">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        11
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      In recent times Waddington's notion of the epigenetic landscape has been rigorously formalized in the context of the
      <a href="/wiki/System_dynamics" title="System dynamics">
       systems dynamics
      </a>
      state approach to the study of cell-fate.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12">
       <a href="#cite_note-12">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        12
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sciencedirect.com_13-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-sciencedirect.com-13">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        13
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Cell-fate determination is predicted to exhibit certain dynamics, such as attractor-convergence (the attractor can be an equilibrium point, limit cycle or
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Strange_attractor" title="Strange attractor">
       strange attractor
      </a>
      ) or oscillatory.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sciencedirect.com_13-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-sciencedirect.com-13">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        13
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      The term "epigenetics" has also been used in
      <a href="/wiki/Developmental_psychology" title="Developmental psychology">
       developmental psychology
      </a>
      to describe psychological development as the result of an ongoing, bi-directional interchange between heredity and the environment.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14">
       <a href="#cite_note-14">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        14
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Interactivist ideas of development have been discussed in various forms and under various names throughout the 19th and 20th centuries. An early version was proposed, among the founding statements in
      <a href="/wiki/Embryology" title="Embryology">
       embryology
      </a>
      , by
      <a href="/wiki/Karl_Ernst_von_Baer" title="Karl Ernst von Baer">
       Karl Ernst von Baer
      </a>
      and popularized by
      <a href="/wiki/Ernst_Haeckel" title="Ernst Haeckel">
       Ernst Haeckel
      </a>
      . A radical epigenetic view (physiological epigenesis) was developed by
      <a href="/wiki/Paul_Wintrebert" title="Paul Wintrebert">
       Paul Wintrebert
      </a>
      . Another variation, probabilistic epigenesis, was presented by
      <a href="/wiki/Gilbert_Gottlieb" title="Gilbert Gottlieb">
       Gilbert Gottlieb
      </a>
      in 2003.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15">
       <a href="#cite_note-15">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        15
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      This view encompasses all of the possible developing factors on an organism and how they not only influence the organism and each other, but how the organism also influences its own development.
     </p>
     <p>
      The developmental psychologist
      <a href="/wiki/Erik_Erikson" title="Erik Erikson">
       Erik Erikson
      </a>
      used the term
      <b>
       epigenetic principle
      </b>
      in his book
      <a href="/wiki/Erik_Erikson#Bibliography" title="Erik Erikson">
       <i>
        Identity: Youth and Crisis
       </i>
       (1968)
      </a>
      , and used it to encompass the notion that we develop through an unfolding of our personality in predetermined stages, and that our environment and surrounding culture influence how we progress through these stages. This biological unfolding in relation to our socio-cultural settings is done in
      <a href="/wiki/Erikson%27s_stages_of_psychosocial_development" title="Erikson's stages of psychosocial development">
       stages of psychosocial development
      </a>
      , where "progress through each stage is in part determined by our success, or lack of success, in all the previous stages."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Boeree1997_16-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Boeree1997-16">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        16
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17">
       <a href="#cite_note-17">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        17
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18">
       <a href="#cite_note-18">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        18
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Contemporary_usage">
       Contemporary usage
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Epigenetics&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Contemporary usage">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Epigenetic_mechanisms.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="485" data-file-width="700" height="208" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/Epigenetic_mechanisms.jpg/300px-Epigenetic_mechanisms.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/Epigenetic_mechanisms.jpg/450px-Epigenetic_mechanisms.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/Epigenetic_mechanisms.jpg/600px-Epigenetic_mechanisms.jpg 2x" width="300"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Epigenetic_mechanisms.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Epigenetic mechanisms
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      <a href="/wiki/Robin_Holliday" title="Robin Holliday">
       Robin Holliday
      </a>
      defined epigenetics as "the study of the mechanisms of temporal and spatial control of gene activity during the development of complex organisms."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid2265224_19-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-pmid2265224-19">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        19
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Thus
      <i>
       epigenetic
      </i>
      can be used to describe anything other than DNA sequence that influences the development of an organism.
     </p>
     <p>
      The more recent usage of the word in science has a stricter definition. It is, as defined by
      <a href="/wiki/Arthur_Riggs_(geneticist)" title="Arthur Riggs (geneticist)">
       Arthur Riggs
      </a>
      and colleagues, "the study of
      <a href="/wiki/Mitosis" title="Mitosis">
       mitotically
      </a>
      and/or
      <a href="/wiki/Meiosis" title="Meiosis">
       meiotically
      </a>
      heritable changes in gene function that cannot be explained by changes in DNA sequence."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-isbn0-87969-490-4_20-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-isbn0-87969-490-4-20">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        20
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The Greek prefix
      <i>
       epi-
      </i>
      in
      <i>
       epigenetics
      </i>
      implies features that are "on top of" or "in addition to" genetics; thus
      <i>
       epigenetic
      </i>
      traits exist on top of or in addition to the traditional molecular basis for inheritance.
     </p>
     <p>
      The term "epigenetics", however, has been used to describe processes which have not been demonstrated to be heritable such as histone modification; there are therefore attempts to redefine it in broader terms that would avoid the constraints of requiring heritability. For example,
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Adrian_Peter_Bird" title="Adrian Peter Bird">
       Sir Adrian Bird
      </a>
      defined epigenetics as
      <i>
       "the structural adaptation of chromosomal regions so as to register, signal or perpetuate altered activity states."
      </i>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid17522671_5-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-pmid17522671-5">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        5
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      This definition would be inclusive of transient modifications associated with
      <a href="/wiki/DNA_repair" title="DNA repair">
       DNA repair
      </a>
      or
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cell-cycle" title="Cell-cycle">
       cell-cycle
      </a>
      phases as well as stable changes maintained across multiple cell generations, but exclude others such as templating of membrane architecture and
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Prions" title="Prions">
       prions
      </a>
      unless they impinge on chromosome function. Such redefinitions however are not universally accepted and are still subject to dispute.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nature2008_2-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-nature2008-2">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        2
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The NIH "Roadmap Epigenomics Project," ongoing as of 2013, uses the following definition:
      <i>
       "...For purposes of this program, epigenetics refers to both heritable changes in gene activity and expression (in the progeny of cells or of individuals) and also stable, long-term alterations in the transcriptional potential of a cell that are not necessarily heritable."
      </i>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NIH_21-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-NIH-21">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        21
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      In 2008, a consensus definition of the epigenetic trait, "stably heritable phenotype resulting from changes in a chromosome without alterations in the DNA sequence", was made at a
      <a href="/wiki/Cold_Spring_Harbor_Laboratory" title="Cold Spring Harbor Laboratory">
       Cold Spring Harbor
      </a>
      meeting.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid19339683_22-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-pmid19339683-22">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        22
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      The similarity of the word to "genetics" has generated many parallel usages. The "epigenome" is a parallel to the word "
      <a href="/wiki/Genome" title="Genome">
       genome
      </a>
      ", referring to the overall epigenetic state of a cell, and
      <a href="/wiki/Epigenomics" title="Epigenomics">
       epigenomics
      </a>
      refers to more global analyses of epigenetic changes across the entire genome.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NIH_21-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-NIH-21">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        21
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The phrase "
      <a href="/wiki/Genetic_code" title="Genetic code">
       genetic code
      </a>
      " has also been adaptedthe "
      <a href="/wiki/Epigenetic_code" title="Epigenetic code">
       epigenetic code
      </a>
      " has been used to describe the set of epigenetic features that create different phenotypes in different cells. Taken to its extreme, the "epigenetic code" could represent the total state of the cell, with the position of each molecule accounted for in an
      <i>
       epigenomic map
      </i>
      , a diagrammatic representation of the gene expression, DNA methylation and histone modification status of a particular genomic region. More typically, the term is used in reference to systematic efforts to measure specific, relevant forms of epigenetic information such as the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Histone_code_hypothesis" title="Histone code hypothesis">
       histone code
      </a>
      or
      <a href="/wiki/DNA_methylation" title="DNA methylation">
       DNA methylation
      </a>
      patterns.
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Molecular_basis">
       Molecular basis
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Epigenetics&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Molecular basis">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <p>
      Epigenetic changes can modify the activation of certain genes, but not the sequence of
      <a href="/wiki/DNA" title="DNA">
       DNA
      </a>
      . Additionally, the
      <a href="/wiki/Chromatin" title="Chromatin">
       chromatin
      </a>
      proteins associated with DNA may be activated or silenced. This is why the differentiated cells in a multicellular organism express only the genes that are necessary for their own activity. Epigenetic changes are preserved when cells divide. Most epigenetic changes only occur within the course of one individual organism's lifetime, but, if gene inactivation occurs in a sperm or egg cell that results in fertilization, then some epigenetic changes can be transferred to the next generation.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid17320501_23-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-pmid17320501-23">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        23
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      This raises the question of whether or not epigenetic changes in an organism can alter the basic structure of its DNA (see
      <a href="#Evolution">
       Evolution, below
      </a>
      ), a form of
      <a href="/wiki/Lamarckism" title="Lamarckism">
       Lamarckism
      </a>
      .
     </p>
     <p>
      Specific epigenetic processes include
      <a href="/wiki/Paramutation" title="Paramutation">
       paramutation
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Bookmarking" title="Bookmarking">
       bookmarking
      </a>
      ,
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Imprinting_(genetics)" title="Imprinting (genetics)">
       imprinting
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Gene_silencing" title="Gene silencing">
       gene silencing
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/X-inactivation" title="X-inactivation">
       X chromosome inactivation
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Position_effect" title="Position effect">
       position effect
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Reprogramming" title="Reprogramming">
       reprogramming
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Transvection_(genetics)" title="Transvection (genetics)">
       transvection
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Maternal_effect" title="Maternal effect">
       maternal effects
      </a>
      , the progress of
      <a href="/wiki/Carcinogenesis" title="Carcinogenesis">
       carcinogenesis
      </a>
      , many effects of
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Teratogen" title="Teratogen">
       teratogens
      </a>
      , regulation of
      <a href="/wiki/Histone" title="Histone">
       histone
      </a>
      modifications and
      <a href="/wiki/Heterochromatin" title="Heterochromatin">
       heterochromatin
      </a>
      , and technical limitations affecting
      <a href="/wiki/Parthenogenesis" title="Parthenogenesis">
       parthenogenesis
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Cloning" title="Cloning">
       cloning
      </a>
      .
     </p>
     <p>
      DNA damage can also cause epigenetic changes.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24">
       <a href="#cite_note-24">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        24
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25">
       <a href="#cite_note-25">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        25
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26">
       <a href="#cite_note-26">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        26
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      DNA damages are very frequent, occurring on average about 10,000 times a day per cell of the human body (see
      <a href="/wiki/DNA_damage_(naturally_occurring)" title="DNA damage (naturally occurring)">
       DNA damage (naturally occurring)
      </a>
      ). These damages are largely repaired, but at the site of a DNA repair, epigenetic changes can remain.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27">
       <a href="#cite_note-27">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        27
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      In particular, a double strand break in DNA can initiate unprogrammed epigenetic gene silencing both by causing DNA methylation as well as by promoting silencing types of histone modifications (chromatin remodeling) (see next section).
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28">
       <a href="#cite_note-28">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        28
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      In addition, the enzyme
      <a href="/wiki/Poly_ADP_ribose_polymerase" title="Poly ADP ribose polymerase">
       Parp1 (poly(ADP)-ribose polymerase)
      </a>
      and its product poly(ADP)-ribose (PAR) accumulate at sites of DNA damage as part of a repair process.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29">
       <a href="#cite_note-29">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        29
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      This accumulation, in turn, directs recruitment and activation of the chromatin remodeling protein ALC1 that can cause nucleosome remodeling.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30">
       <a href="#cite_note-30">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        30
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Nucleosome remodeling has been found to cause, for instance, epigenetic silencing of DNA repair gene MLH1.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-isbn0-87969-490-4_20-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-isbn0-87969-490-4-20">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        20
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31">
       <a href="#cite_note-31">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        31
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      DNA damaging chemicals, such as
      <a href="/wiki/Benzene" title="Benzene">
       benzene
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Hydroquinone" title="Hydroquinone">
       hydroquinone
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Styrene" title="Styrene">
       styrene
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Carbon_tetrachloride" title="Carbon tetrachloride">
       carbon tetrachloride
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Trichloroethylene" title="Trichloroethylene">
       trichloroethylene
      </a>
      , cause considerable hypomethylation of DNA, some through the activation of oxidative stress pathways.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32">
       <a href="#cite_note-32">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        32
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Foods are known to alter the epigenetics of rats on different diets.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33">
       <a href="#cite_note-33">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        33
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Some food components epigenetically increase the levels of DNA repair enzymes such as
      <a href="/wiki/O-6-methylguanine-DNA_methyltransferase" title="O-6-methylguanine-DNA methyltransferase">
       MGMT
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/MLH1" title="MLH1">
       MLH1
      </a>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34">
       <a href="#cite_note-34">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        34
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/P53" title="P53">
       p53
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35">
       <a href="#cite_note-35">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        35
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36">
       <a href="#cite_note-36">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        36
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Other food components can reduce DNA damage, such as soy
      <a href="/wiki/Isoflavones" title="Isoflavones">
       isoflavones
      </a>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37">
       <a href="#cite_note-37">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        37
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38">
       <a href="#cite_note-38">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        38
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      and bilberry anthocyanins.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39">
       <a href="#cite_note-39">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        39
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Epigenetic research uses a wide range of molecular biologic techniques to further our understanding of epigenetic phenomena, including
      <a href="/wiki/Chromatin_immunoprecipitation" title="Chromatin immunoprecipitation">
       chromatin immunoprecipitation
      </a>
      (together with its large-scale variants
      <a href="/wiki/ChIP-on-chip" title="ChIP-on-chip">
       ChIP-on-chip
      </a>
      and
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ChIP-Seq" title="ChIP-Seq">
       ChIP-Seq
      </a>
      ),
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fluorescent_in_situ_hybridization" title="Fluorescent in situ hybridization">
       fluorescent in situ hybridization
      </a>
      , methylation-sensitive
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Restriction_enzymes" title="Restriction enzymes">
       restriction enzymes
      </a>
      , DNA adenine methyltransferase identification (
      <a href="/wiki/DamID" title="DamID">
       DamID
      </a>
      ) and
      <a href="/wiki/Bisulfite_sequencing" title="Bisulfite sequencing">
       bisulfite sequencing
      </a>
      . Furthermore, the use of
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bioinformatic" title="Bioinformatic">
       bioinformatic
      </a>
      methods is playing an increasing role (
      <a href="/wiki/Computational_epigenetics" title="Computational epigenetics">
       computational epigenetics
      </a>
      ).
     </p>
     <p>
      Computer simulations and molecular dynamics approaches revealed the atomistic motions associated with the molecular recognition of the histone tail through an allosteric mechanism.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid22802671_40-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-pmid22802671-40">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        40
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Mechanisms">
       Mechanisms
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Epigenetics&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Mechanisms">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <p>
      Several types of epigenetic inheritance systems may play a role in what has become known as cell memory,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jablonka92_41-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-jablonka92-41">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        41
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      note however that not all of these are universally accepted to be examples of epigenetics.
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Covalent_modifications_of_DNA_and_histones">
       Covalent modifications of DNA and histones
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Epigenetics&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Covalent modifications of DNA and histones">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Covalent" title="Covalent">
       Covalent
      </a>
      modifications of DNA (e.g. cytosine methylation and hydroxymethylation) and histone proteins (e.g. lysine acetylation, lysine and arginine methylation, serine and threonine phosphorylation, and lysine ubiquitination and sumoylation) play central roles in many types of epigenetic inheritance. Therefore, the word "epigenetics" is sometimes used as a synonym for these processes. However, this can be misleading. Chromatin remodeling is not always inherited, and not all epigenetic inheritance involves chromatin remodeling.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid17407749_42-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-pmid17407749-42">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        42
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Nucleosome_1KX5_2.png">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1024" data-file-width="1024" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/db/Nucleosome_1KX5_2.png/220px-Nucleosome_1KX5_2.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/db/Nucleosome_1KX5_2.png/330px-Nucleosome_1KX5_2.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/db/Nucleosome_1KX5_2.png/440px-Nucleosome_1KX5_2.png 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Nucleosome_1KX5_2.png" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        DNA associates with histone proteins to form chromatin.
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      Because the
      <a href="/wiki/Phenotype" title="Phenotype">
       phenotype
      </a>
      of a cell or individual is affected by which of its genes are transcribed, heritable
      <a href="/wiki/Transcription_(genetics)" title="Transcription (genetics)">
       transcription states
      </a>
      can give rise to epigenetic effects. There are several layers of regulation of
      <a href="/wiki/Gene_expression" title="Gene expression">
       gene expression
      </a>
      . One way that genes are regulated is through the remodeling of chromatin. Chromatin is the complex of DNA and the
      <a href="/wiki/Histone" title="Histone">
       histone
      </a>
      proteins with which it associates. If the way that DNA is wrapped around the histones changes, gene expression can change as well. Chromatin remodeling is accomplished through two main mechanisms:
     </p>
     <ol>
      <li>
       The first way is
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Post_translational_modification" title="Post translational modification">
        post translational modification
       </a>
       of the amino acids that make up histone proteins. Histone proteins are made up of long chains of amino acids. If the amino acids that are in the chain are changed, the shape of the histone might be modified. DNA is not completely unwound during replication. It is possible, then, that the modified histones may be carried into each new copy of the DNA. Once there, these histones may act as templates, initiating the surrounding new histones to be shaped in the new manner. By altering the shape of the histones around them, these modified histones would ensure that a lineage-specific transcription program is maintained after cell division.
      </li>
      <li>
       The second way is the addition of methyl groups to the DNA, mostly at
       <a href="/wiki/CpG_site" title="CpG site">
        CpG sites
       </a>
       , to convert
       <a href="/wiki/Cytosine" title="Cytosine">
        cytosine
       </a>
       to
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/5-methylcytosine" title="5-methylcytosine">
        5-methylcytosine
       </a>
       . 5-Methylcytosine performs much like a regular cytosine, pairing with a guanine in double-stranded DNA. However, some areas of the genome are methylated more heavily than others, and highly methylated areas tend to be less transcriptionally active, through a mechanism not fully understood. Methylation of cytosines can also persist from the germ line of one of the parents into the zygote, marking the chromosome as being inherited from one parent or the other (
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Genetic_imprinting" title="Genetic imprinting">
        genetic imprinting
       </a>
       ).
      </li>
     </ol>
     <p>
      Mechanisms of heritability of histone state are not well understood; however, much is known about the mechanism of heritability of DNA methylation state during cell division and differentiation. Heritability of methylation state depends on certain enzymes (such as
      <a href="/wiki/DNA_methyltransferase" title="DNA methyltransferase">
       DNMT1
      </a>
      ) that have a higher affinity for 5-methylcytosine than for cytosine. If this enzyme reaches a "hemimethylated" portion of DNA (where 5-methylcytosine is in only one of the two DNA strands) the enzyme will methylate the other half.
     </p>
     <p>
      Although histone modifications occur throughout the entire sequence, the unstructured N-termini of histones (called histone tails) are particularly highly modified. These modifications include
      <a href="/wiki/Acetylation" title="Acetylation">
       acetylation
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Methylation" title="Methylation">
       methylation
      </a>
      ,
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ubiquitylation" title="Ubiquitylation">
       ubiquitylation
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Phosphorylation" title="Phosphorylation">
       phosphorylation
      </a>
      ,
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sumoylation" title="Sumoylation">
       sumoylation
      </a>
      , ribosylation and citrullination. Acetylation is the most highly studied of these modifications. For example, acetylation of the K14 and K9
      <a href="/wiki/Lysine" title="Lysine">
       lysines
      </a>
      of the tail of histone H3 by histone acetyltransferase enzymes (HATs) is generally related to transcriptional competence.
     </p>
     <p>
      One mode of thinking is that this tendency of acetylation to be associated with "active" transcription is biophysical in nature. Because it normally has a positively charged nitrogen at its end, lysine can bind the negatively charged phosphates of the DNA backbone. The acetylation event converts the positively charged amine group on the side chain into a neutral amide linkage. This removes the positive charge, thus loosening the DNA from the histone. When this occurs, complexes like
      <a href="/wiki/SWI/SNF" title="SWI/SNF">
       SWI/SNF
      </a>
      and other transcriptional factors can bind to the DNA and allow transcription to occur. This is the "cis" model of epigenetic function. In other words, changes to the histone tails have a direct effect on the DNA itself.
     </p>
     <p>
      Another model of epigenetic function is the "trans" model. In this model, changes to the histone tails act indirectly on the DNA. For example, lysine acetylation may create a binding site for chromatin-modifying enzymes (or transcription machinery as well). This chromatin remodeler can then cause changes to the state of the chromatin. Indeed, a bromodomain  a protein domain that specifically binds acetyl-lysine  is found in many enzymes that help activate transcription, including the
      <a href="/wiki/SWI/SNF" title="SWI/SNF">
       SWI/SNF
      </a>
      complex. It may be that acetylation acts in this and the previous way to aid in transcriptional activation.
     </p>
     <p>
      The idea that modifications act as docking modules for related factors is borne out by
      <a href="/wiki/Histone_methylation" title="Histone methylation">
       histone methylation
      </a>
      as well. Methylation of lysine 9 of histone H3 has long been associated with constitutively transcriptionally silent chromatin (constitutive
      <a href="/wiki/Heterochromatin" title="Heterochromatin">
       heterochromatin
      </a>
      ). It has been determined that a chromodomain (a domain that specifically binds methyl-lysine) in the transcriptionally repressive protein
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Heterochromatin_Protein_1" title="Heterochromatin Protein 1">
       HP1
      </a>
      recruits HP1 to K9 methylated regions. One example that seems to refute this biophysical model for methylation is that tri-methylation of histone H3 at lysine 4 is strongly associated with (and required for full) transcriptional activation. Tri-methylation in this case would introduce a fixed positive charge on the tail.
     </p>
     <p>
      It has been shown that the histone lysine methyltransferase (KMT) is responsible for this methylation activity in the pattern of histones H3 &amp; H4. This enzyme utilizes a catalytically active site called the SET domain (Suppressor of variegation, Enhancer of zeste, Trithorax). The SET domain is a 130-amino acid sequence involved in modulating gene activities. This domain has been demonstrated to bind to the histone tail and causes the methylation of the histone.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid9487389_43-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-pmid9487389-43">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        43
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Differing histone modifications are likely to function in differing ways; acetylation at one position is likely to function differently from acetylation at another position. Also, multiple modifications may occur at the same time, and these modifications may work together to change the behavior of the nucleosome. The idea that multiple dynamic modifications regulate gene transcription in a systematic and reproducible way is called the
      <a href="/wiki/Histone_code" title="Histone code">
       histone code
      </a>
      , although the idea that histone state can be read linearly as a digital information carrier has been largely debunked. One of the best-understood systems that orchestrates chromatin-based silencing is the
      <a href="/wiki/SIR_protein" title="SIR protein">
       SIR protein
      </a>
      based silencing of the yeast hidden mating type loci HML and HMR.
     </p>
     <p>
      DNA methylation frequently occurs in repeated sequences, and helps to suppress the expression and mobility of '
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Transposable_elements" title="Transposable elements">
       transposable elements
      </a>
      ':
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-slotkin2007_44-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-slotkin2007-44">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        44
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Because
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/5-methylcytosine" title="5-methylcytosine">
       5-methylcytosine
      </a>
      can be spontaneously deaminated (replacing nitrogen by oxygen) to
      <a href="/wiki/Thymidine" title="Thymidine">
       thymidine
      </a>
      , CpG sites are frequently mutated and become rare in the genome, except at
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/CpG_islands" title="CpG islands">
       CpG islands
      </a>
      where they remain unmethylated. Epigenetic changes of this type thus have the potential to direct increased frequencies of permanent genetic mutation.
      <a href="/wiki/DNA_methylation" title="DNA methylation">
       DNA methylation
      </a>
      patterns are known to be established and modified in response to environmental factors by a complex interplay of at least three independent
      <a href="/wiki/DNA_methyltransferase" title="DNA methyltransferase">
       DNA methyltransferases
      </a>
      , DNMT1, DNMT3A, and DNMT3B, the loss of any of which is lethal in mice.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-li92_45-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-li92-45">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        45
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      DNMT1 is the most abundant methyltransferase in somatic cells,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-robertson99_46-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-robertson99-46">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        46
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      localizes to replication foci,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-leonhardt92_47-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-leonhardt92-47">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        47
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      has a 1040-fold preference for hemimethylated DNA and interacts with the
      <a href="/wiki/Proliferating_cell_nuclear_antigen" title="Proliferating cell nuclear antigen">
       proliferating cell nuclear antigen
      </a>
      (PCNA).
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-chuang97_48-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-chuang97-48">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        48
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      By preferentially modifying hemimethylated DNA, DNMT1 transfers patterns of methylation to a newly synthesized strand after
      <a href="/wiki/DNA_replication" title="DNA replication">
       DNA replication
      </a>
      , and therefore is often referred to as the maintenance' methyltransferase.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-robertson00_49-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-robertson00-49">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        49
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      DNMT1 is essential for proper embryonic development, imprinting and X-inactivation.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-li92_45-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-li92-45">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        45
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-li93_50-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-li93-50">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        50
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      To emphasize the difference of this molecular mechanism of inheritance from the canonical Watson-Crick base-pairing mechanism of transmission of genetic information, the term 'Epigenetic templating' was introduced.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51">
       <a href="#cite_note-51">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        51
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Furthermore, in addition to the maintenance and transmission of methylated DNA states, the same principle could work in the maintenance and transmission of histone modifications and even cytoplasmic (
      <a href="/wiki/Structural_inheritance" title="Structural inheritance">
       structural
      </a>
      ) heritable states.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid18419815_52-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-pmid18419815-52">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        52
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Histones H3 and H4 can also be manipulated through demethylation using histone lysine demethylase (KDM). This recently identified enzyme has a catalytically active site called the Jumonji domain (JmjC). The demethylation occurs when JmjC utilizes multiple cofactors to hydroxylate the methyl group, thereby removing it. JmjC is capable of demethylating mono-, di-, and tri-methylated substrates.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid19234061_53-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-pmid19234061-53">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        53
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Chromosomal regions can adopt stable and heritable alternative states resulting in bistable gene expression without changes to the DNA sequence. Epigenetic control is often associated with alternative
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Covalent_modification" title="Covalent modification">
       covalent modifications
      </a>
      of histones.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rosenfeld_2009_54-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Rosenfeld_2009-54">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        54
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The stability and heritability of states of larger chromosomal regions are suggested to involve positive feedback where modified nucleosomes recruit enzymes that similarly modify nearby nucleosomes.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55">
       <a href="#cite_note-55">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        55
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      A simplified stochastic model for this type of epigenetics is found here.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56">
       <a href="#cite_note-56">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        56
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid17512413_57-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-pmid17512413-57">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        57
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      It has been suggested that chromatin-based transcriptional regulation could be mediated by the effect of small RNAs.
      <a href="/wiki/Small_interfering_RNA" title="Small interfering RNA">
       Small interfering RNAs
      </a>
      can modulate transcriptional gene expression via epigenetic modulation of targeted
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Promoter_(biology)" title="Promoter (biology)">
       promoters
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Morris_58-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Morris-58">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        58
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="RNA_transcripts_and_their_encoded_proteins">
       RNA transcripts and their encoded proteins
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Epigenetics&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: RNA transcripts and their encoded proteins">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      Sometimes a gene, after being turned on, transcribes a product that (directly or indirectly) maintains the activity of that gene. For example,
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hnf4" title="Hnf4">
       Hnf4
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/MyoD" title="MyoD">
       MyoD
      </a>
      enhance the transcription of many liver- and muscle-specific genes, respectively, including their own, through the
      <a href="/wiki/Transcription_factor" title="Transcription factor">
       transcription factor
      </a>
      activity of the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Proteins" title="Proteins">
       proteins
      </a>
      they encode. RNA signalling includes differential recruitment of a hierarchy of generic chromatin modifying complexes and DNA methyltransferases to specific loci by RNAs during differentiation and development.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid19154003_59-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-pmid19154003-59">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        59
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Other epigenetic changes are mediated by the production of
      <a href="/wiki/Alternative_splicing" title="Alternative splicing">
       different splice forms
      </a>
      of
      <a href="/wiki/RNA" title="RNA">
       RNA
      </a>
      , or by formation of double-stranded RNA (
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/RNAi" title="RNAi">
       RNAi
      </a>
      ). Descendants of the cell in which the gene was turned on will inherit this activity, even if the original stimulus for gene-activation is no longer present. These genes are often turned on or off by
      <a href="/wiki/Signal_transduction" title="Signal transduction">
       signal transduction
      </a>
      , although in some systems where
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Syncytia" title="Syncytia">
       syncytia
      </a>
      or
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gap_junctions" title="Gap junctions">
       gap junctions
      </a>
      are important, RNA may spread directly to other cells or nuclei by
      <a href="/wiki/Diffusion" title="Diffusion">
       diffusion
      </a>
      . A large amount of RNA and protein is contributed to the
      <a href="/wiki/Zygote" title="Zygote">
       zygote
      </a>
      by the mother during
      <a href="/wiki/Oogenesis" title="Oogenesis">
       oogenesis
      </a>
      or via
      <a href="/wiki/Nurse_cell" title="Nurse cell">
       nurse cells
      </a>
      , resulting in
      <a href="/wiki/Maternal_effect" title="Maternal effect">
       maternal effect
      </a>
      phenotypes. A smaller quantity of sperm RNA is transmitted from the father, but there is recent evidence that this epigenetic information can lead to visible changes in several generations of offspring.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-choi06_60-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-choi06-60">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        60
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="MicroRNAs">
       MicroRNAs
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Epigenetics&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: MicroRNAs">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      <a href="/wiki/MicroRNA" title="MicroRNA">
       MicroRNAs
      </a>
      (miRNAs) are members of
      <a href="/wiki/Non-coding_RNA" title="Non-coding RNA">
       non-coding RNAs
      </a>
      that range in size from 17 to 25 nucleotides. miRNAs regulate a large variety of biological functions in plants and animals.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wang_61-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Wang-61">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        61
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      So far, in 2013, about 2000 miRNAs have been discovered in humans and these can be found online in an miRNA database.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62">
       <a href="#cite_note-62">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        62
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Each miRNA expressed in a cell may target about 100 to 200 messenger RNAs that it downregulates.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63">
       <a href="#cite_note-63">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        63
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Most of the downregulation of mRNAs occurs by causing the decay of the targeted mRNA, while some downregulation occurs at the level of translation into protein.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64">
       <a href="#cite_note-64">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        64
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      It appears that about 60% of human protein coding genes are regulated by miRNAs.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65">
       <a href="#cite_note-65">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        65
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Many miRNAs are epigenetically regulated. About 50% of miRNA genes are associated with
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/CpG_island" title="CpG island">
       CpG islands
      </a>
      ,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wang_61-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Wang-61">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        61
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      that may be repressed by epigenetic methylation. Transcription from methylated CpG islands is strongly and heritably repressed.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66">
       <a href="#cite_note-66">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        66
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Other miRNAs are epigenetically regulated by either histone modifications or by combined DNA methylation and histone modification.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wang_61-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-Wang-61">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        61
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="mRNA">
       mRNA
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Epigenetics&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: mRNA">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      In 2011, it was demonstrated that the
      <a href="/wiki/Methylation" title="Methylation">
       methylation
      </a>
      of
      <a href="/wiki/Messenger_RNA" title="Messenger RNA">
       mRNA
      </a>
      plays a critical role in human
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Energy_balance_(biology)" title="Energy balance (biology)">
       energy homeostasis
      </a>
      . The obesity-associated
      <a href="/wiki/FTO_gene" title="FTO gene">
       FTO gene
      </a>
      is shown to be able to
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Demethylate" title="Demethylate">
       demethylate
      </a>
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/N6-methyladenosine" title="N6-methyladenosine">
       N6-methyladenosine
      </a>
      in RNA.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67">
       <a href="#cite_note-67">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        67
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68">
       <a href="#cite_note-68">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        68
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="sRNAs">
       sRNAs
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Epigenetics&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: sRNAs">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      <a href="/wiki/Bacterial_small_RNA" title="Bacterial small RNA">
       sRNAs
      </a>
      are small (50250 nucleotides), highly structured, non-coding RNA fragments found in bacteria. They control gene expression including
      <a href="/wiki/Virulence" title="Virulence">
       virulence
      </a>
      genes in pathogens and are viewed as new targets in the fight against drug-resistant bacteria.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69">
       <a href="#cite_note-69">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        69
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      They play an important role in many biological processes, binding to mRNA and protein targets in prokaryotes. Their phylogenetic analyses, for example through sRNAmRNA target interactions or protein
      <a href="/wiki/Hfq_binding_sRNA" title="Hfq binding sRNA">
       binding properties
      </a>
      , are used to build comprehensive databases.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70">
       <a href="#cite_note-70">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        70
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      sRNA-
      <a href="/wiki/Gene_map" title="Gene map">
       gene maps
      </a>
      based on their targets in microbial genomes are also constructed.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71">
       <a href="#cite_note-71">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        71
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Prions">
       Prions
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Epigenetics&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Prions">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="hatnote">
      For more details on this topic, see
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fungal_prions" title="Fungal prions">
       Fungal prions
      </a>
      .
     </div>
     <p>
      <a href="/wiki/Prion" title="Prion">
       Prions
      </a>
      are
      <a href="/wiki/Infection" title="Infection">
       infectious
      </a>
      forms of
      <a href="/wiki/Protein" title="Protein">
       proteins
      </a>
      . In general, proteins fold into discrete units that perform distinct cellular functions, but some proteins are also capable of forming an infectious conformational state known as a prion. Although often viewed in the context of
      <a href="/wiki/Transmissible_spongiform_encephalopathy" title="Transmissible spongiform encephalopathy">
       infectious disease
      </a>
      , prions are more loosely defined by their ability to catalytically convert other native state versions of the same protein to an infectious conformational state. It is in this latter sense that they can be viewed as epigenetic agents capable of inducing a phenotypic change without a modification of the genome.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72">
       <a href="#cite_note-72">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        72
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      <a href="/wiki/Fungal_prion" title="Fungal prion">
       Fungal prions
      </a>
      are considered by some to be epigenetic because the infectious phenotype caused by the prion can be inherited without modification of the genome.
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/PSI_(prion)" title="PSI (prion)">
       PSI+
      </a>
      and URE3, discovered in
      <a href="/wiki/Saccharomyces_cerevisiae" title="Saccharomyces cerevisiae">
       yeast
      </a>
      in 1965 and 1971, are the two best studied of this type of prion.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73">
       <a href="#cite_note-73">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        73
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid5573734_74-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-pmid5573734-74">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        74
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Prions can have a phenotypic effect through the sequestration of protein in aggregates, thereby reducing that protein's activity. In PSI+ cells, the loss of the Sup35 protein (which is involved in termination of translation) causes ribosomes to have a higher rate of read-through of stop
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Codon" title="Codon">
       codons
      </a>
      , an effect that results in suppression of
      <a href="/wiki/Nonsense_mutation" title="Nonsense mutation">
       nonsense mutations
      </a>
      in other genes.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid225301_75-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-pmid225301-75">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        75
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The ability of Sup35 to form prions may be a conserved trait. It could confer an adaptive advantage by giving cells the ability to
      <a href="/wiki/Evolutionary_capacitance" title="Evolutionary capacitance">
       switch into a PSI+ state
      </a>
      and express dormant genetic features normally terminated by stop codon mutations.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid11028992_76-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-pmid11028992-76">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        76
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid15931169_77-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-pmid15931169-77">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        77
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78">
       <a href="#cite_note-78">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        78
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79">
       <a href="#cite_note-79">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        79
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Structural_inheritance_systems">
       Structural inheritance systems
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Epigenetics&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Structural inheritance systems">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="hatnote">
      For more details on this topic, see
      <a href="/wiki/Structural_inheritance" title="Structural inheritance">
       Structural inheritance
      </a>
      .
     </div>
     <p>
      In
      <a href="/wiki/Ciliate" title="Ciliate">
       ciliates
      </a>
      such as
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Tetrahymena" title="Tetrahymena">
        Tetrahymena
       </a>
      </i>
      and
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Paramecium" title="Paramecium">
        Paramecium
       </a>
      </i>
      , genetically identical cells show heritable differences in the patterns of ciliary rows on their cell surface. Experimentally altered patterns can be transmitted to daughter cells. It seems existing structures act as templates for new structures. The mechanisms of such inheritance are unclear, but reasons exist to assume that multicellular organisms also use existing cell structures to assemble new ones.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid1804215_80-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-pmid1804215-80">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        80
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-isbn0-19-515619-6_81-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-isbn0-19-515619-6-81">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        81
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-isbn0-262-65063-0_82-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-isbn0-262-65063-0-82">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        82
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Nucleosome_positioning">
       Nucleosome positioning
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Epigenetics&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Nucleosome positioning">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      Eukaryotic genomes are packed with the help of
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nucleosomes" title="Nucleosomes">
       nucleosomes
      </a>
      . Nucleosome positions are not random, and determine the accessibility of DNA to regulatory proteins. This determines differences in gene expression and cell differentiation. It has been shown that at least some nucleosomes are retained in sperm cells (where most but not all histones are replaced by
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Protamines" title="Protamines">
       protamines
      </a>
      ). Thus nucleosome positioning is to some degree inheritable. Recent studies have uncovered connections between nucleosome positioning and other epigenetic factors, such as DNA methylation and hydroxymethylation
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Teif_2014_83-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Teif_2014-83">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        83
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Functions_and_consequences">
       Functions and consequences
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Epigenetics&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Functions and consequences">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Development">
       Development
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Epigenetics&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Development">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      Somatic epigenetic inheritance, particularly through DNA and histone covalent modifications and nucleosome repositioning, is very important in the development of multicellular eukaryotic organisms.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Teif_2014_83-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Teif_2014-83">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        83
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The genome sequence is static (with some notable exceptions), but cells differentiate into many different types, which perform different functions, and respond differently to the environment and intercellular signalling. Thus, as individuals develop,
      <a href="/wiki/Morphogen" title="Morphogen">
       morphogens
      </a>
      activate or silence genes in an epigenetically heritable fashion, giving cells a memory. In mammals, most cells terminally differentiate, with only
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Stem_cells" title="Stem cells">
       stem cells
      </a>
      retaining the ability to differentiate into several cell types ("totipotency" and "multipotency"). In
      <a href="/wiki/Mammal" title="Mammal">
       mammals
      </a>
      , some stem cells continue producing new differentiated cells throughout life, such as in
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Epigenetic_Regulation_of_Neurogenesis" title="Epigenetic Regulation of Neurogenesis">
       neurogenesis
      </a>
      , but mammals are not able to respond to loss of some tissues, for example, the inability to regenerate limbs, which some other animals are capable of. Epigenetic modifications regulate the transition from neural neural stem cells to glial progenitor cells (for example, differentiation into oligodendrocytes is regulated by the deacetylation and methylation of histones.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84">
       <a href="#cite_note-84">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        84
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Unlike animals, plant cells do not terminally differentiate, remaining totipotent with the ability to give rise to a new individual plant. While plants do utilise many of the same epigenetic mechanisms as animals, such as
      <a href="/wiki/Chromatin_remodeling" title="Chromatin remodeling">
       chromatin remodeling
      </a>
      , it has been hypothesised that some kinds of plant cells do not use or require "cellular memories", resetting their gene expression patterns using positional information from the environment and surrounding cells to determine their fate.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid17194589_85-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-pmid17194589-85">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        85
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Epigenetics can be divided into predetermined and probabilistic epigenesis. Predetermined epigenesis is a unidirectional movement from structural development in DNA to the functional maturation of the protein. "Predetermined" here means that development is scripted and predictable. Probabilistic epigenesis on the other hand is a bidirectional structure-function development with experiences and external molding development.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Griesemer_2005_86-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Griesemer_2005-86">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        86
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Medicine">
       Medicine
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Epigenetics&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Medicine">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      Epigenetics has many and varied potential medical applications as it tends to be multidimensional in nature.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid21447282_87-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-pmid21447282-87">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        87
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Congenital genetic disease is well understood and it is clear that epigenetics can play a role, for example, in the case of
      <a href="/wiki/Angelman_syndrome" title="Angelman syndrome">
       Angelman syndrome
      </a>
      and
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Prader-Willi_syndrome" title="Prader-Willi syndrome">
       Prader-Willi syndrome
      </a>
