      . These are normal genetic diseases caused by gene deletions or inactivation of the genes, but are unusually common because individuals are essentially
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hemizygous" title="Hemizygous">
       hemizygous
      </a>
      because of
      <a href="/wiki/Genomic_imprinting" title="Genomic imprinting">
       genomic imprinting
      </a>
      , and therefore a single gene knock out is sufficient to cause the disease, where most cases would require both copies to be knocked out.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88">
       <a href="#cite_note-88">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        88
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Evolution">
       Evolution
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Epigenetics&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Evolution">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="hatnote">
      See also:
      <a href="/wiki/Transgenerational_epigenetics" title="Transgenerational epigenetics">
       Transgenerational epigenetics
      </a>
     </div>
     <p>
      Epigenetics can impact evolution when epigenetic changes are heritable. A sequestered germ line or
      <a href="/wiki/Weismann_barrier" title="Weismann barrier">
       Weismann barrier
      </a>
      is specific to animals, and epigenetic inheritance is more common in plants and microbes.
      <a href="/wiki/Eva_Jablonka" title="Eva Jablonka">
       Eva Jablonka
      </a>
      and
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Marion_Lamb" title="Marion Lamb">
       Marion Lamb
      </a>
      have argued that these effects may require enhancements to the standard conceptual framework of the
      <a href="/wiki/Modern_evolutionary_synthesis" title="Modern evolutionary synthesis">
       modern evolutionary synthesis
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-isbn0-262-10107-6_89-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-isbn0-262-10107-6-89">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        89
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90">
       <a href="#cite_note-90">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        90
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Other evolutionary biologists have incorporated epigenetic inheritance into
      <a href="/wiki/Population_genetics" title="Population genetics">
       population genetics
      </a>
      models
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91">
       <a href="#cite_note-91">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        91
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      or are openly skeptical.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92">
       <a href="#cite_note-92">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        92
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Two important ways in which epigenetic inheritance can be different from traditional genetic inheritance, with important consequences for evolution, are that rates of epimutation can be much faster than rates of mutation
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rando_and_verstrepen_93-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-rando_and_verstrepen-93">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        93
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      and the epimutations are more easily reversible.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94">
       <a href="#cite_note-94">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        94
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      In plants heritable DNA methylation mutations are 100.000 times more likely to occur compared to DNA mutations.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-van_der_Graaf_et_al_95-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-van_der_Graaf_et_al-95">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        95
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      An epigenetically inherited element such as the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/PSI_(prion)" title="PSI (prion)">
       PSI+
      </a>
      system can act as a "stop-gap", good enough for short-term adaptation that allows the lineage to survive for long enough for mutation and/or recombination to
      <a href="/wiki/Genetic_assimilation" title="Genetic assimilation">
       genetically assimilate
      </a>
      the adaptive phenotypic change.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96">
       <a href="#cite_note-96">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        96
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The existence of this possibility increases the
      <a href="/wiki/Evolvability" title="Evolvability">
       evolvability
      </a>
      of a species.
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Current_research_findings_and_examples_of_effects">
       Current research findings and examples of effects
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Epigenetics&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Current research findings and examples of effects">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      Epigenetic changes have been observed to occur in response to environmental exposurefor example, mice given some dietary supplements have epigenetic changes affecting expression of the
      <a href="/wiki/Agouti_gene" title="Agouti gene">
       agouti gene
      </a>
      , which affects their fur color, weight, and propensity to develop cancer.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid12163699_97-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-pmid12163699-97">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        97
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-waterland_98-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-waterland-98">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        98
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      One study indicates that traumatic experiences can produce fearful memories which are passed to future generations via epigenetics. A study carried out on mice in 2013 found that mice could produce offspring which had an aversion to certain items which had been the source of negative experiences for their ancestors.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99">
       <a href="#cite_note-99">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        99
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100">
       <a href="#cite_note-100">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        100
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Reports stated that:
     </p>
     <blockquote>
      <p>
       For the study, author Brian Dias and co-author Kerry Ressler trained mice, using foot shocks, to fear an odour that resembles cherry blossoms. Later, they tested the extent to which the animals' offspring startled when exposed to the same smell. The younger generation had not even been conceived when their fathers underwent the training, and had never smelt the odour before the experiment.
      </p>
      <p>
       The offspring of trained mice were "able to detect and respond to far less amounts of odour... suggesting they are more sensitive" to it, Ressler told AFP of the findings published in the journal Nature Neuroscience. They did not react the same way to other odours, and compared to the offspring of non-trained mice, their reaction to the cherry blossom whiff was about 200 percent stronger, he said.
      </p>
      The scientists then looked at a gene, M71, that governs the functioning of an odour receptor in the nose that responds specifically to the cherry blossom smell. The gene, inherited through the sperm of trained mice, had undergone no change to its DNA encoding, the team found. But the gene did carry epigenetic marks that could alter its behaviour and cause it to be "expressed more" in descendants, said Dias. This in turn caused a physical change in the brains of the trained mice, their sons and grandsons, who all had a larger glomerulusa section in the olfactory (smell) unit of the brain.
     </blockquote>
     <p>
      This study received several criticisms. In a paper in GENETICS, author Gregory Francis cited the study's low statistical power as evidence of some irregularity such as bias in reporting results.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101">
       <a href="#cite_note-101">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        101
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Due to limits of sample size, there is a probability that an effect will not be demonstrated to within statistical significance even if it exists. Francis calculated the probability that all the experiments reported would show positive results if an identical protocol was followed, assuming the claimed effects exist, to be 0.4%. Another criticism was that the authors did not indicate which mice were siblings, and treated all of the mice as statistically independent.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102">
       <a href="#cite_note-102">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        102
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Dias and Ressler responded by pointing out negative results in the paper's appendix that Francis did not use in his calculations, and by saying they would track which mice were siblings in the future.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103">
       <a href="#cite_note-103">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        103
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      In the case of humans with different environmental exposures, monozygotic (identical) twins were epigenetically indistinguishable during their early years, while older twins had remarkable differences in the overall content and genomic distribution of 5-methylcytosine DNA and histone acetylation. The twin pairs who had spent less of their lifetime together and/or had greater differences in their medical histories were those who showed the largest differences in their levels of 5-methylcytosine DNA and acetylation of histones H3 and H4.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid16009939_104-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-pmid16009939-104">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        104
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      More than 100cases of
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Transgenerational_epigenetic_inheritance" title="Transgenerational epigenetic inheritance">
       transgenerational epigenetic inheritance
      </a>
      phenomena have been reported in a wide range of organisms, including prokaryotes, plants, and animals.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jablonka09_105-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Jablonka09-105">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        105
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      For instance,
      <a href="/wiki/Nymphalis_antiopa" title="Nymphalis antiopa">
       Mourning Cloak butterflies
      </a>
      will change color through hormone changes in response to experimentation of varying temperatures.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106">
       <a href="#cite_note-106">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        106
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Recent analyses have suggested that members of the APOBEC/AID family of cytosine deaminases are capable of simultaneously mediating genetic and epigenetic inheritance using similar molecular mechanisms.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid20800313_107-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-pmid20800313-107">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        107
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Epigenetic_effects_in_humans">
       Epigenetic effects in humans
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Epigenetics&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Epigenetic effects in humans">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Genomic_imprinting_and_related_disorders">
       Genomic imprinting and related disorders
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Epigenetics&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Genomic imprinting and related disorders">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      Some human disorders are associated with
      <a href="/wiki/Genomic_imprinting" title="Genomic imprinting">
       genomic imprinting
      </a>
      , a phenomenon in mammals where the father and mother contribute different epigenetic patterns for specific genomic loci in their
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Germ_cells" title="Germ cells">
       germ cells
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid17121465_108-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-pmid17121465-108">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        108
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The best-known case of imprinting in human disorders is that of
      <a href="/wiki/Angelman_syndrome" title="Angelman syndrome">
       Angelman syndrome
      </a>
      and
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Prader-Willi_syndrome" title="Prader-Willi syndrome">
       Prader-Willi syndrome
      </a>
      both can be produced by the same genetic mutation,
      <a href="/wiki/Chromosome_15q_partial_deletion" title="Chromosome 15q partial deletion">
       chromosome 15q partial deletion
      </a>
      , and the particular syndrome that will develop depends on whether the mutation is inherited from the child's mother or from their father.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid2564739_109-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-pmid2564739-109">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        109
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      This is due to the presence of genomic imprinting in the region.
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Beckwith-Wiedemann_syndrome" title="Beckwith-Wiedemann syndrome">
       Beckwith-Wiedemann syndrome
      </a>
      is also associated with genomic imprinting, often caused by abnormalities in maternal genomic imprinting of a region on chromosome 11.
     </p>
     <p>
      <a href="/wiki/Rett_syndrome" title="Rett syndrome">
       Rett syndrome
      </a>
      is underlied by mutations in the
      <a href="/wiki/MECP2" title="MECP2">
       MECP2
      </a>
      gene despite no large-scale changes in expression of MeCP2 being found in microarray analyses.
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/BDNF" title="BDNF">
       BDNF
      </a>
      is downregulated in the MECP2 mutant resulting in Rett syndrome.
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Transgenerational_epigenetic_observations">
       Transgenerational epigenetic observations
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Epigenetics&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Transgenerational epigenetic observations">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">
      Main article:
      <a href="/wiki/Transgenerational_epigenetics" title="Transgenerational epigenetics">
       Transgenerational epigenetics
      </a>
     </div>
     <p>
      In the
      <a href="/wiki/%C3%96verkalix_study" title="verkalix study">
       verkalix study
      </a>
      , Marcus Pembrey and colleagues observed that the paternal (but not maternal) grandsons
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-paternal-grandson_110-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-paternal-grandson-110">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        110
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      of Swedish men who were exposed during preadolescence to famine in the 19th century were less likely to die of cardiovascular disease. If food was plentiful, then
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Diabetes" title="Diabetes">
       diabetes
      </a>
      mortality in the grandchildren increased, suggesting that this was a transgenerational epigenetic inheritance.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid16391557_111-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-pmid16391557-111">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        111
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The opposite effect was observed for femalesthe paternal (but not maternal) granddaughters of women who experienced famine while in the womb (and therefore while their eggs were being formed) lived shorter lives on average.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112">
       <a href="#cite_note-112">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        112
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Cancer_and_developmental_abnormalities">
       Cancer and developmental abnormalities
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Epigenetics&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Cancer and developmental abnormalities">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">
      Main article:
      <a href="/wiki/Cancer_epigenetics" title="Cancer epigenetics">
       Cancer epigenetics
      </a>
     </div>
     <p>
      A variety of compounds are considered as epigenetic
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Carcinogens" title="Carcinogens">
       carcinogens
      </a>
      they result in an increased incidence of tumors, but they do not show
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mutagenic" title="Mutagenic">
       mutagen
      </a>
      activity (toxic compounds or pathogens that cause tumors incident to increased regeneration should also be excluded). Examples include
      <a href="/wiki/Diethylstilbestrol" title="Diethylstilbestrol">
       diethylstilbestrol
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Arsenite" title="Arsenite">
       arsenite
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Hexachlorobenzene" title="Hexachlorobenzene">
       hexachlorobenzene
      </a>
      , and
      <a href="/wiki/Nickel" title="Nickel">
       nickel
      </a>
      compounds.
     </p>
     <p>
      Many teratogens exert specific effects on the fetus by epigenetic mechanisms.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bishop97_113-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-bishop97-113">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        113
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gurvich04_114-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-gurvich04-114">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        114
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      While epigenetic effects may preserve the effect of a teratogen such as
      <a href="/wiki/Diethylstilbestrol" title="Diethylstilbestrol">
       diethylstilbestrol
      </a>
      throughout the life of an affected child, the possibility of birth defects resulting from exposure of fathers or in second and succeeding generations of offspring has generally been rejected on theoretical grounds and for lack of evidence.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-smithells98_115-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-smithells98-115">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        115
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      However, a range of male-mediated abnormalities have been demonstrated, and more are likely to exist.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-friedler96_116-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-friedler96-116">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        116
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      FDA label information for Vidaza, a formulation of
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/5-azacytidine" title="5-azacytidine">
       5-azacitidine
      </a>
      (an unmethylatable analog of cytidine that causes hypomethylation when incorporated into DNA) states that "men should be advised not to father a child" while using the drug, citing evidence in treated male mice of reduced fertility, increased embryo loss, and abnormal embryo development.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-117">
       <a href="#cite_note-117">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        117
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      In rats, endocrine differences were observed in offspring of males exposed to morphine.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cicero91_118-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-cicero91-118">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        118
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      In mice, second generation effects of diethylstilbesterol have been described occurring by epigenetic mechanisms.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-newbold06_119-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-newbold06-119">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        119
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Recent studies have shown that the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/MLL_(gene)" title="MLL (gene)">
       mixed-lineage leukemia
      </a>
      (MLL) gene causes
      <a href="/wiki/Leukemia" title="Leukemia">
       leukemia
      </a>
      by rearranging and fusing with other genes in different chromosomes, which is a process under epigenetic control.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid20236314_120-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-pmid20236314-120">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        120
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Other investigations have concluded that alterations in histone acetylation and DNA methylation occur in various genes influencing prostate cancer.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Li2005_121-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Li2005-121">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        121
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Gene expression in the prostate can be modulated by nutrition and lifestyle changes.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid18559852_122-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-pmid18559852-122">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        122
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      In 2008, the National Institutes of Health announced that $190 million had been earmarked for epigenetics research over the next five years. In announcing the funding, government officials noted that epigenetics has the potential to explain mechanisms of aging, human development, and the origins of cancer, heart disease, mental illness, as well as several other conditions. Some investigators, like
      <a href="/wiki/Randy_Jirtle" title="Randy Jirtle">
       Randy Jirtle
      </a>
      , PhD, of Duke University Medical Center, think epigenetics may ultimately turn out to have a greater role in disease than genetics.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-123">
       <a href="#cite_note-123">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        123
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h4>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="DNA_methylation_in_cancer">
       DNA methylation in cancer
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Epigenetics&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: DNA methylation in cancer">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h4>
     <p>
      <a href="/wiki/DNA_methylation" title="DNA methylation">
       DNA methylation
      </a>
      is an important regulator of gene transcription and a large body of evidence has demonstrated that aberrant DNA methylation is associated with unscheduled gene silencing, and the genes with high levels of 5-methylcytosine in their promoter region are transcriptionally silent. DNA methylation is essential during embryonic development, and in somatic cells, patterns of DNA methylation are in general transmitted to daughter cells with a high fidelity. Aberrant DNA methylation patterns have been associated with a large number of human malignancies and found in two distinct forms: hypermethylation and hypomethylation compared to normal tissue. Hypermethylation is one of the major epigenetic modifications that repress transcription via promoter region of tumour suppressor genes. Hypermethylation typically occurs at CpG islands in the promoter region and is associated with gene inactivation. Global hypomethylation has also been implicated in the development and progression of cancer through different mechanisms.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CraigJMWongNC_124-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-CraigJMWongNC-124">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        124
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h4>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="DNA_repair_epigenetics_in_cancer">
       DNA repair epigenetics in cancer
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Epigenetics&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: DNA repair epigenetics in cancer">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h4>
     <p>
      Germ line (familial) mutations have been identified in 34 different DNA repair genes that cause a high risk of cancer, including, for example
      <a href="/wiki/BRCA1" title="BRCA1">
       BRCA1
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Ataxia_telangiectasia_mutated" title="Ataxia telangiectasia mutated">
       ATM
      </a>
      . These are listed in the article
      <a href="/wiki/DNA_repair-deficiency_disorder" title="DNA repair-deficiency disorder">
       DNA repair-deficiency disorder
      </a>
      . However, cancers caused by such germ line mutations make up only a very small proportion of cancers. For instance, germ line mutations cause only 2% to 5% of colon cancer cases.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-125">
       <a href="#cite_note-125">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        125
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Epigenetic reductions in expression of DNA repair genes, however, are very frequent in sporadic (non-germ line) cancers, as shown among some representative cancers in the table in this section, while mutations in DNA repair genes in sporadic cancer are very rare.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-126">
       <a href="#cite_note-126">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        126
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>

        <a href="/wiki/Ataxia_telangiectasia_mutated" title="Ataxia telangiectasia mutated">
         ATM
        </a>
       </td>

        CpG island methylation
       </td>
       <td>
        25%
       </td>
       <td>
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-table_18_146-0">
         <a href="#cite_note-table_18-146">

          </span>
          146
          <span>
           ]
          </span>
         </a>
        </sup>
       </td>
      </tr>
     </table>
     <p>
      Deficiencies in expression of DNA repair genes cause increased mutation rates. Mutations rates increase in mice defective for mismatch DNA repair genes
      <a href="/wiki/PMS2" title="PMS2">
       PMS2
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/MLH1" title="MLH1">
       MLH1
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/MSH2" title="MSH2">
       MSH2
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/MSH3" title="MSH3">
       MSH3
      </a>
      or
      <a href="/wiki/MSH6" title="MSH6">
       MSH6
      </a>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-147">
       <a href="#cite_note-147">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        147
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-148">
       <a href="#cite_note-148">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        148
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      or for DNA repair gene
      <a href="/wiki/BRCA2" title="BRCA2">
       BRCA2
      </a>
      ,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid11850397_149-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-pmid11850397-149">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        149
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      while chromosomal rearrangements and aneuploidy are noted to increase in humans defective in DNA repair gene
      <a href="/wiki/Bloom_syndrome_protein" title="Bloom syndrome protein">
       BLM
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-150">
       <a href="#cite_note-150">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        150
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Thus, deficiency in DNA repair causes
      <a href="/wiki/Genome_instability" title="Genome instability">
       genome instability
      </a>
      and this
      <a href="/wiki/Genome_instability" title="Genome instability">
       genome instability
      </a>
      is likely the main underlying cause of the genetic alterations leading to cancer. In fact, the first event in many sporadic neoplasias is a heritable alteration that affects genetic instability and epigenetic defects in DNA repair are somatically heritable.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-151">
       <a href="#cite_note-151">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        151
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h4>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Variant_histones_H2A_in_cancer">
       Variant histones H2A in cancer
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Epigenetics&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Variant histones H2A in cancer">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h4>
     <p>
      The
      <a href="/wiki/Histone" title="Histone">
       histone
      </a>
      variants of the H2A family are highly conserved in mammals, playing critical roles in regulating many nuclear processes by altering
      <a href="/wiki/Chromatin" title="Chromatin">
       chromatin
      </a>
      structure. One of the key H2A variants, H2A.X, marks DNA damage, facilitating the recruitment of DNA repair proteins to restore genomic integrity. Another variant, H2A.Z, plays an important role in both gene activation and repression. A high level of H2A.Z expression is ubiquitously detected in many cancers and is significantly associated with cellular proliferation and genomic instability.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CraigJMWongNC_124-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-CraigJMWongNC-124">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        124
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Histone variant macroH2A1 is important in the pathogenesis of many types of cancers, for instance in hepatocellular carcinoma.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-152">
       <a href="#cite_note-152">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        152
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h4>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Cancer_treatment">
       Cancer treatment
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Epigenetics&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Cancer treatment">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h4>
     <p>
      Current research has shown that epigenetic pharmaceuticals could be a replacement or adjuvant therapy for currently accepted treatment methods such as
      <a href="/wiki/Radiation_therapy" title="Radiation therapy">
       radiation
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Chemotherapy" title="Chemotherapy">
       chemotherapy
      </a>
      , or could enhance the effects of these current treatments.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid20664922_153-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-pmid20664922-153">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        153
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      It has been shown that the epigenetic control of the proto-onco regions and the tumor suppressor sequences by conformational changes in histones directly affects the formation and progression of cancer.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IglesiasLinares2010_154-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-IglesiasLinares2010-154">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        154
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Epigenetics also has the factor of reversibility, a characteristic that other cancer treatments do not offer.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Li2005_121-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Li2005-121">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        121
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Drug development has focused mainly on
      <a href="/wiki/Histone_acetyltransferase" title="Histone acetyltransferase">
       histone acetyltransferase
      </a>
      (HAT) and
      <a href="/wiki/Histone_deacetylase" title="Histone deacetylase">
       histone deacetylase
      </a>
      (HDAC), and has included the introduction to the market of the new pharmaceutical
      <a href="/wiki/Vorinostat" title="Vorinostat">
       vorinostat
      </a>
      , an HDAC inhibitor.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid18773966_155-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-pmid18773966-155">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        155
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      HDAC has been shown to play an integral role in the progression of oral squamous cancer.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IglesiasLinares2010_154-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-IglesiasLinares2010-154">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        154
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      The
      <a href="/wiki/Lunasin" title="Lunasin">
       lunasin
      </a>
      seed molecule histone has also shown promise at creating epigenetic effects and early studies indicate chemopreventive properties.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AJC_156-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-AJC-156">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        156
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Current front-runner candidates for new parmaceutical targets are
      <a href="/wiki/Histone_methyltransferase" title="Histone methyltransferase">
       histone lysine methyltransferases
      </a>
      (KMT) and protein arginine methyltransferases (PRMT).
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid20219369_157-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-pmid20219369-157">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        157
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Twin_studies">
       Twin studies
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Epigenetics&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Twin studies">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      Recent studies involving both dizygotic (fraternal) and monozygotic (identical) twins have produced some evidence of epigenetic influence in humans.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid16009939_104-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-pmid16009939-104">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        104
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid19151718_158-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-pmid19151718-158">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        158
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-159">
       <a href="#cite_note-159">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        159
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Direct comparisons between identical twins constitute the ideal experimental model for testing environmental epigenetics, because DNA sequence differences that would be abundant in a singleton-based study do not interfere with the analysis. Research has shown that a difference in the environment can produce long-term epigenetic effects, and different developmental monozygotic twin subtypes may be different with respect to their susceptibility to be discordant from an epigenetic point of view.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid19653134_160-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-pmid19653134-160">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        160
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      One of the first high-throughput studies of epigenetic differences between monozygotic twins focused in comparing global and locus-specific changes in DNA methylation and histone modifications in a sample of 40 monozygotic twin pairs.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid16009939_104-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-pmid16009939-104">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        104
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      In this case, only healthy twin pairs were studied, but a wide range of ages was represented, between 3 and 74 years. One of the major conclusions from this study was that there is an age-dependent accumulation of epigenetic differences between the two siblings of twin pairs. This accumulation suggests the existence of epigenetic drift.
     </p>
     <p>
      A more recent study, where 114 monozygotic twins and 80 dizygotic twins were analyzed for the DNA methylation status of around 6000 unique genomic regions, concluded that epigenetic similarity at the time of blastocyst splitting may also contribute to phenotypic similarities in monozygotic co-twins. This supports the notion that microenvironment at early stages of embryonic development can be quite important for the establishment of epigenetic marks.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-161">
       <a href="#cite_note-161">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        161
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Epigenetics_in_microorganisms">
       Epigenetics in microorganisms
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Epigenetics&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Epigenetics in microorganisms">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Escherichia_coli_flagella_TEM.png">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="896" data-file-width="600" height="224" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/eb/Escherichia_coli_flagella_TEM.png/150px-Escherichia_coli_flagella_TEM.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/eb/Escherichia_coli_flagella_TEM.png/225px-Escherichia_coli_flagella_TEM.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/eb/Escherichia_coli_flagella_TEM.png/300px-Escherichia_coli_flagella_TEM.png 2x" width="150"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Escherichia_coli_flagella_TEM.png" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Escherichia coli bacteria
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      Bacteria make widespread use of postreplicative DNA methylation for the epigenetic control of DNA-protein interactions. Bacteria make use of DNA
      <a href="/wiki/Adenine" title="Adenine">
       adenine
      </a>
      methylation (rather than DNA
      <a href="/wiki/Cytosine" title="Cytosine">
       cytosine
      </a>
      methylation) as an epigenetic signal. DNA adenine methylation is important in bacteria virulence in organisms such as
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Escherichia_coli" title="Escherichia coli">
        Escherichia coli
       </a>
      </i>
      ,
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Salmonella" title="Salmonella">
        Salmonella
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Vibrio" title="Vibrio">
        Vibrio
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Yersinia" title="Yersinia">
        Yersinia
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Haemophilus" title="Haemophilus">
        Haemophilus
       </a>
      </i>
      , and
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Brucella" title="Brucella">
        Brucella
       </a>
      </i>
      . In
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Alphaproteobacteria" title="Alphaproteobacteria">
        Alphaproteobacteria
       </a>
      </i>
      , methylation of adenine regulates the cell cycle and couples gene transcription to DNA replication. In
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Gammaproteobacteria" title="Gammaproteobacteria">
        Gammaproteobacteria
       </a>
      </i>
      , adenine methylation provides signals for DNA replication, chromosome segregation, mismatch repair, packaging of bacteriophage, transposase activity and regulation of gene expression.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Casadesus_162-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Casadesus-162">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        162
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-JorgTost_163-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-JorgTost-163">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        163
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      The filamentous fungus
      <i>
       Neurospora crassa
      </i>
      is a prominent model system for understanding the control and function of cytosine methylation. In this organisms, DNA methylation is associated with relics of a genome defense system called RIP (repeat-induced point mutation) and silences gene expression by inhibiting transcription elongation.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid19092133_164-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-pmid19092133-164">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        164
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      The
      <a href="/wiki/Yeast" title="Yeast">
       yeast
      </a>
      <a href="/wiki/Prion" title="Prion">
       prion
      </a>
      PSI is generated by a conformational change of a translation termination factor, which is then inherited by daughter cells. This can provide a survival advantage under adverse conditions. This is an example of epigenetic regulation enabling unicellular organisms to respond rapidly to environmental stress. Prions can be viewed as epigenetic agents capable of inducing a phenotypic change without modification of the genome.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-JorgTost_163-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-JorgTost-163">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        163
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Direct detection of epigenetic marks in microorganisms is possible with
      <a href="/wiki/Single_molecule_real_time_sequencing" title="Single molecule real time sequencing">
       single molecule real time sequencing
      </a>
      , in which polymerase sensitivity allows for measuring methylation and other modifications as a DNA molecule is being sequenced.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-165">
       <a href="#cite_note-165">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        165
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Several projects have demonstrated the ability to collect genome-wide epigenetic data in bacteria.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-166">
       <a href="#cite_note-166">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        166
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-167">
       <a href="#cite_note-167">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        167
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-168">
       <a href="#cite_note-168">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        168
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-169">
       <a href="#cite_note-169">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        169
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="In_Popular_Culture">
       In Popular Culture
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Epigenetics&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: In Popular Culture">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <p>
      In
      <a href="/wiki/Neal_Stephenson" title="Neal Stephenson">
       Neal Stephenson
      </a>
      s 2015 novel
      <a href="/wiki/Seveneves" title="Seveneves">
       Seveneves
      </a>
      , survivors of a worldwide holocaust are tasked with seeding new life on a dormant Earth. Rather than create specific breeds of animals to be hunters, scavengers, or prey, species like canids are developed with mutable epigenetic traits, with the intention that the animals would quickly transform into the necessary roles that would be required for life to better and more quickly evolved. Additionally, a race of humans, Moirans, are created to survive in space, with the hope that this subspecies of human would be able to adapt to unforeseeable dangers and circumstances.
     </p>

        <span class="reference-text">

          Spector, Tim (2012).
          <i>
           Identically Different: Why You Can Change Your Genes
          </i>
          . London:

          </a>

          <q>
           Just over ten years ago researchers found that the diets of
           <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pregnant" title="Pregnant">
            pregnant
           </a>
           <a href="/wiki/Mother" title="Mother">
            mothers
           </a>
           could alter the behaviour of
           <a href="/wiki/Gene" title="Gene">
            genes
           </a>
           in their children and that these changes could last a lifetime and then be passed on in turn to their children. The genes were literally being switched on or off by a new mechanism we call epigenetics  meaning in Greek 'around the gene'. Contrary to traditional genetic dogma, these changes could be transferred to the next generation. In this case the mothers just happened to be rats, but recent similar findings in humans have created a revolution in our thinking.
          </q>

         <span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AEpigenetics&amp;rft.atitle=Stability+and+flexibility+of+epigenetic+gene+regulation+in+mammalian+development&amp;rft.aufirst=W&amp;rft.aulast=Reik&amp;rft.au=Reik%2C+W&amp;rft.date=May+2007&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft_id=info%3Abibcode%2F2007Natur.447..425R&amp;rft_id=info%3Adoi%2F10.1038%2Fnature05918&amp;rft_id=info%3Apmid%2F17522676&amp;rft.issue=7143&amp;rft.jtitle=Nature&amp;rft.pages=425-32&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.volume=447">
          <span style="display:none;">
          </span>
         </span>
        </span>
       </li>
       <li id="cite_note-waddington-8">
        <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
         <b>
          <a href="#cite_ref-waddington_8-0">
           ^
          </a>
         </b>
        </span>
        <span class="reference-text">
         <span class="citation journal">
          Waddington CH (1942). "The epigenotype".
          <i>
           Endeavour
          </i>
          <b>
           1
          </b>
          : 1820.
         </span>
         <span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AEpigenetics&amp;rft.atitle=The+epigenotype&amp;rft.aulast=Waddington+CH&amp;rft.au=Waddington+CH&amp;rft.date=1942&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft.jtitle=Endeavour&amp;rft.pages=18-20&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.volume=1">
          <span style="display:none;">
          </span>
         </span>
        </span>
       </li>
       <li id="cite_note-OED1-9">
        <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
         <b>
          <a href="#cite_ref-OED1_9-0">
           ^
          </a>
         </b>
        </span>
        <span class="reference-text">
         According to the
         <a href="/wiki/Oxford_English_Dictionary" title="Oxford English Dictionary">
          Oxford English Dictionary
         </a>
         :
        </span>
        <blockquote class="templatequote">
         <p>
          <span class="reference-text">
           The word is used by W. Harvey,
           <i>
            Exercitationes
           </i>
           1651, p. 148, and in the
           <i>
            English Anatomical Exercitations
           </i>
           1653, p. 272. It is explained to mean partium super-exorientium additamentum, the additament of parts budding one out of another.
          </span>
         </p>
        </blockquote>
        <span class="reference-text">
         It is also worth quoting this adumbration of the definition given there (viz., "The formation of an organic germ as a new product"):
        </span>
        <blockquote class="templatequote">
         <p>
          <span class="reference-text">
           <b>
            theory of epigenesis
           </b>
           : the theory that the germ is brought into existence (by successive accretions), and not merely developed, in the process of reproduction. [...] The opposite theory was formerly known as the theory of evolution; to avoid the ambiguity of this name, it is now spoken of chiefly as the theory of preformation, sometimes as that of encasement or embotement.
          </span>
         </p>
        </blockquote>
       </li>
